SUMMARY OF FINDINGS
The geotechnical engineering exploration
indicates that the proposed transit alignment will
cross a variety of subsurface conditions
Therefore at least three different foundation
systems will probably be required to support the
fixed guideway system
This exploration was conducted to provide
general data for preliminary design It is
recommended that additional geotechnical
engineering exploration and analyses be
conducted as part of the final design efforts of
the proposed transit system
The text of this report should be referred to for
detailed discussions and findings
INTRODUCTION
A geotechnical engineering exploration of the aerial fixed guideway system portions
of the proposed Honolulu Rapid Transit System alignment was conducted This report
summarizes our work on the project and presents our findings
Additional laboratory test results on selected samples will be submitted in an
addendum to this report on
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 2
This exploration was conducted to provide preliminary design information The
findings and recommendations presented herein are not intended for final design nor are
they intended for construction planning and final cost estimation
PROJECT CONSIDERATIONS
This report provides geotechnical information along the aerial alignment of the fixed
guideway Rapid Transit system from Waiau Interchange to the University of Hawaii at
Manoa and to Waikiki The geotechnical information for the subway section of the
alignment under Hotel Street from Kaaahi Street to Dreier Street is not included in this
report
The proposed alignment runs above ground from Waiawa at the U S Navy Ewa
Drum Storage Site along Kamehameha Highway past Aloha Stadium and Honolulu
Internationai Airport to Keehi Interchange and Dillingham Boulevard The selected route
then follows Dillingham Boulevard to North King Street becoming an underground system
at Kaaahi Street and proceeding under Hotel Street to Kapiolani Boulevard at Alapai and
South Streets At Dreier Street the system again surfaces above ground and proceeds
along Waimanu and Kona Streets past Ala Moana Shopping Center to Kalakaua Avenue
The system follows Kapiolani Boulevard to University Avenue and then along University
Avenue to the University of Hawaii at Manoa At Kalakaua the Waikiki Line will follow
Kalakaua and Kuhio Avenues to Kapahulu Avenue
This report also provides geotechnical information along Kapiolani Boulevard from
South Street to Kalakaua Avenue
Under the project development concept the majority of the proposed transit
system will be an elevated fixed guideway system with the guideway supported by and
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 3
Suspended between piers Relatively large vertical loads large live loads and high
overturning moments are typically generated by these long span structures
In addition to the fixed guideway system there will be related structures such as
passenger stations The current plans for the proposed transit system indicate that
twenty one 21 aerial and three 8 subway passenger stations are planned along the
system route
This report was prepared solely to provide preliminary subsurface design
information The details of the actual transit system structures are not known at this time
therefore this report does not contain sufficient data nor appropriate recommendations
for final detailed design of the transit system foundations
PURPOSE AND SCOPE
The purpose of our exploration was to obtain an overview of the subsurface soil
conditions along the aerial rail system portions of the proposed transit system alignments
to develop a generalized soils data set for the general information of and use by the
system contractor This report is not intended for use in the final design of the transit
system foundations nor is it intended for final construction planning and cost estimation
The scope of our work on this project included but was not iimited to the following
elements
1 A review of some of the available soils and geological literature maps and
soils reports published and unpublished pertinent to the proposed transit
system corridor and its vicinity This review was addressed in a previous
reconnaissance report dated August 31 1991
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 4
2 The drilling and sampling of 59 borings at selected locations along the
proposed alignment
3 Coordination of the field work and logging of the borings by field engineers
and geologists from our firm
4 Laboratory testing of selected samples obtained during the field exploration
to aid in classifying the materials encountered and to evaluate their
engineering properties
5 Analyses of the field and laboratory data to formulate preliminary
geotechnical engineering recommendations pertinent to the design of the
aerial portions of the subject project
6 Client consultation attendance of meetings and overall project coordination
7 Preparation of this report summarizing our work on the project and
presenting our findings and recommendations
REGIONAL GEOLOGY
The island of Oahu is composed largely of the weathered remnants of two extinct
shield volcanoes Waianae and Koolau The older voicano Waianae forms the bulk of
the western third of the island while the younger shield Koolau forms the majority of the
eastern two thirds of the island Waianae became extinct while Koolau was still active and
its eastern flank is partially buried below Koolau lavas in central Oahu
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 5
The proposed transit system corridor is iocated along the southern flank of Koolau
and its geomorphology and subsurface conditions are directly related to glacio eustatic
fluctuations of sea level during the Pleistocene Epoch and the genesis of Pear Harbor
and the Honolulu Coastal Plain The subsurface conditions along the majority of the
alignments are dominated by fossil Pleistocene Age coral algal reef deposits and related
deposits laid during high stands of the sea during the Pleistocene Epoch which are
intercalated with terrigenous sediments The base of the stratigraphic section in this area
is Koolau Basalt
The Waianae and Koolau volcanic shields were built during the tate Pliocene Epoch
and the early Pleistocene Epoch by thin bedded basaltic lava flows The main
shield building activities ceased approximately 5 million years ago Evidence from
drilled wells indicates that the island of Oahu has subsided as much as 1200 feet since
the cessation of this early volcanic activity Macdonald and Abbott 1970
During that period of subsidence corai algal reefs began to grow on the southern
coast of Oahu forming broad bays with barrier reefs across the mouth of the bays The
growth of the reefs essentially kept pace with the rate of subsidence A series of lagoons
formed behind the barrier reefs and both terrigenous and marine sediments accumulated
in the lagoons Macdonald and Abboit 1970
During the Pleistocene Epoch Ice Age there were many sea level changes as a
result of widespread glaciation in the continental areas of the world As the great
continental glaciers accumulated the level of the ocean fell since there was less water
available to fill the oceanic basins Conversely as the glaciers receded or melted global
sea levels rose because more water was available The land mass of Oahu remained
essentially stable during these changes and the fluctuations were eustatic in nature
GEOLABS HAWAI
W O 2636 00 Page 6
These glacio eustatic fluctuations resulted in stands of the sea which were both higher
and lower relative to present sea level on Oahu
The higher sea level stands caused the accumulation of deltas and fans of
terrigenous sediments in the heads of the old bays accumuiation of reef deposits at
corresponding higher elevations and lagoonal marine sediments in the quiet waters
protected by fringing reefs
The concurrent growth of reefs and lagoonal sediments may have aiso resulted in
deposits of coral algal limestone and maris within the predominantly lagoonal sedimentary
unit
The lower sea stands caused streams to carve valleys in the sediments and reef
deposits Subaerial exposure of the sediments and calcareous materials caused
consolidation of the soft deltaic materials and lagoonal deposits and induration of the
calcareous reef materials In addition renewed subaerial erosion of upper areas of the
volcanic dome deposited terrigenous alluvial soils under relatively high energy conditions
During periods of no significant sea level changes continued stream action
extended the alluvial deltas and fans seaward and deposited alluvium over the lagoonal
sediments
The geologic history of the project area has been further complicated by the
deposition of recent pyroclastic materials and lava flows from eruptions of the vents of
members of the Honolulu Volcanic Series including Aliamanu Salt Lake Makalapa
Kamanaiki Punchbowl and other minor vents These
post erosional events i e those following the cessation of shield building were
contemporaneous with the Pleistocene Epoch sea level fluctuations
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 7
Many of the eruptions were rather explosive in nature owing to the interaction of
groundwater with the rising magma resulting in steam explosions which expelled large
quantities of pyroclastic material predominantly ash and cinder These deposits of
pyroclastics have consolidated to form volcanic tuff locally referred to as mudrock One
of these eruptions Aliamanu caused a diversion of Halawa Stream to the north of its
original location into the area of what are now the Southeast and Magazine Lochs of Peari
Harbor during the Kaena 95 feet high stand of the sea
The eruptions of the volcanoes blanketed much of
what is now the metropolitan Honolulu area with a fine black cinder locally known as
black sand These eruptions also produced a thick lava flow which filled the bottom of
Manoa Valley
About 15 000 years ago a relatively rapid rise in sea level occurred During that
rise the deep valleys in the project area were drowned In the last 10 000 years or so
sea level has adjusted to its present stand Terrigenous and marine sediments have
continued to accumulate in low energy estuarine or lagoonail environments resulting in
thick deposits of soft harbor sediments along the coast in the areas which were formerly
valleys and drainageways
Land development and reclamation projects within the last 100 years have brought
the Ewa Honolulu area to its present form A great many of those projects were
originally constructed for agricultural residential and or military development Many of
the resulting fills are of poor quality in terms of supporting large structural loads
Subsurface Conditions
The soils and rocks within the project area may be grouped into several major
stratigraphic units The engineering characteristics of these units appear to be reasonably
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 8
consistent These soil units were used on the maps and in the body of this report and
are identified as follows
Tkb
2 Older Alluvium Qa
3 Coral Algal Deposits Qis
4 Honolulu Basalts Qhb
5 Honolulu Tuffs Qhit

SUBSOIL CHARACTERISTICS
Based on our review of the available data previous experience in the areas of the
proposed transit corridors and the borings drilled and sampled for the current exploration
effort the following discussions summarize the general characteristics of the materials
encountered along the transit route
Koolau Basalt Tkb
The Kooiau Basalt is the oldest geologic unit within the project area It generally
consists of interbedded pahoehoe and a a flows of basaltic lavas and may have a mantle
of cobbles and boulders or residual soil overlying the top of the rock
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 9
lt is anticipated that various types of volcanic rock sub components such as
clinker layers and lava tubes could also be encountered
The Koolau Basalt is the aquifer material of the Pear Harbor and Honolulu Artesian
Basins which are the major developed groundwater bodies supplying fresh water for the
Island of Oahu
The aquifer is the permeable lava rock while the upper confining member caprock
is the relatively impermeable alluvial and lagoonal deposits There is no lower confining
member as is the usual case in most aquifers At the bottom the fresh water level is
confined by salt water in accordance with the Ghyben Herzberg Principle which is based
on observations that fresh groundwater floats on saline groundwater in coastal regions
The ratio of densities between fresh and salt water with salinity compared to that of sea
water Causes the salt water to be displaced downward about 40 feet below sea level for
every foot that the fresh basal water table or potentiometric head rises above sea level
Structurally the Kooiau Basalt and its residual soils are among the strongest of all
the units encountered within the study area Therefore the basalt and related residual
soils and saprolites make excellent bearing formations due to their high strength
characteristics in an undisturbed state However its use for foundation support in many
parts of the project area is severely limited by the considerable depths required to reach
the top of the basalt and the artesian conditions expected where the basalt is
encountered at relatively low elevations
In this report and the boring logs presented in Appendix A the following subunits
of Koolau Basalt are used
GEOLABS HAWAITl
W O 2636 00 Page 10
Residual Soil Koolau Basalt
This subunit consists of soils created from the in situ chemical weathering of basic
rock originating from Koolau Basalt These soils generally consist of hard to very
hard red brownish red or reddish brown clayey silts and silty clays The soils are
usually structureless and may have a blocky texture
Saprolite Koolau Basalt
Saprolites are a form of residual soil which exhibit a relict structure from the parent
rock Structural features such as vesicles and joints are visible in saprolites
Saprolite can grade to extremely weathered rock For the purposes of field
classification saprolite can be broken by hand into soil particles whereas extremely
weathered basalt usually cannot be broken down by hand
Clinker Koolau Basalt
Clinker is fragmental basalt found on the tops and usually at the bottom of a a
lava flows Clinker is generally gravel to cobble sized fragments of scoriaceous
rock in a tight packed but open graded structure
Basalt Flow Koolau Basalt
This subunit is used to designate in situ lava formation In this report no effort is
made to differentiate between pahoehoe and a a flows
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 11
Older Alluvium Qa
The Older Alluvium is assigned the position of being the second oldest unit in the
stratigraphic column of the project area even though some of the coral algal reef deposits
may be intercalated with the alluvial deposits
This unit generally overlies the Koolau Basalt and consists of terrigenous sediments
which have been transported by stream action from the upper reaches of the streams
The materials consist mainly of very stiff to hard brown clayey silts and silty clays Gravel
lenses representing buried stream channels are sometimes encountered Portions of the
Older Alluvium unit contain some weathered basalt boulders and cobbles These coarse
grained parts of the unit may also contain artesian water from the basal fresh water
system or as a sub aquifer
The old lagoonal deposits of the Older Alluvium unit consist of marine or lagoonal
sediments which have been desiccated and consolidated into very stiff to hard gray
clayey silts
The old lagoonal deposits consist almost exclusively of clayey silts of rather uniform
texture and grain size with occasional shell fragments
The Older Alluvium materials generally exhibit high strength which leads to a
relatively high bearing capacity Frequently these materials show a moderate to high
shrink swell potential however this is not considered a constraint for the type of
structural systems proposed on this project Structurally the Older Alluvium materials are
considered to be sufficiently competent to support relatively large structural loads with
proper design
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 12
Coral Algal Deposits Qls
This unit consists of fossil remnants of coral algal reefs and their related detrital
debris deposits In Hawaii the detrital deposits tend to be the dominant portion of any
coral rock assemblage
The detrital deposits are weakly to strongly cemented coral debris consisting of
gravel sand and silt sized clastic fragments Some of the coral fragments were dissolved
and the solution cavities filled with cemented and or uncemented fine sands and silts
Due to the granular nature of the majority of the coral algai deposits and the
degree of cementation frequently present this formation generally has high in situ
strength characteristics Structurally the coral algal reef deposits are capable of
supporting heavy foundation loads
In this report and the boring logs presented in Appendix A the following subunits
of coral aigal deposits are used
Coral Algal Detritus
This subunit consists of granular detritus broken from reef formations The soils
are generally medium dense to dense sandy gravels and gravelly sands Silts and
clays are only present in minor or trace amounts Detritus may also be weakly
cemented
Coral Algal Reef Formation
This formation is composed of corals and calcareous algae in their original growth
positions or detrital deposits which have become moderately to well cemented
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 13
Lagoonal Reef Formation
This subunit consists of dendritic or branching corals in their original growth forms
The interstices between the branches of coral have been filled with a fine sandy
organic silt
Filled Cavity
This term is used to describe what appears to be a cavity in a reef formation which
has been filled by loose sand and soft silts
Beachrock
During Jower stands of the sea beach sands and related littoral deposits were
exposed at elevations below current sea level Frequently when calcareous littoral
deposits remain relatively stable for extended periods of time they will become
cemented into a compact sandstone material
Honolulu Basalt Qhb
The Honolulu Basalts generaily consist of interbedded pahoehoe and a a flows of
basalt lavas and may have plastic alluvial clays with cobbles and boulders overlying the
top of the rock
lt is anticipated that various types of volcanic rock sub components such as
clinker layers and lava tubes could also be encountered
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 14
The basalt frequently acts either as a perching or confining member for
groundwater in the Honolulu Aquifer system Therefore groundwater may be
encountered in excavations located within this unit
Structurally this basalt is also one of the strongest of the units encountered within
the study area Therefore the basalt makes an excellent bearing formation due to its high
strength characteristics in an undisturbed state
Residual Soil Honolulu Basalt
This subunit consists of soils created from the in situ chemical weathering of basic
rock originating from vents of the Honolulu Volcanic Series These soils generally
consist of hard to very hard brown brownish red or reddish brown clayey silts and
silty clays The soils are usually structureless and may have a blocky texture
Saprolite Honolulu Basalt
Saprolites are a form of residual soil which exhibit a relict structure from the parent
rock Structural features such as vesicles and joints are visible in saprolites
Saprolite can grade to extremely weathered rock For the purposes of field
classification saprolite can be broken by hand into soil particles whereas extremely
weathered basalt usually cannot be broken down by hand
Clinker Honoijulu Basalt
Clinker is fragmental basalt found on the tops and usually at the bottom of a a
lava flows Clinker is generally composed of gravel to cobble sized fragments of
scoriaceous rock in a tightly packed but open graded structure
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 15
Basalt Flow Honolulu Basalt
This subunit is used to designate in situ lava formation In this report no effort is
made to differentiate between pahoehoe and a a flows
Honolulu Tuffs Qht
Tuff from vents of the Honolulu Volcanic Series consist of volcanic ash which
consolidated or hardened to form a medium hard rock that resembles siltstone or
sandstone locally called mudrock The tuff is frequently found overlying coralline
deposits or alluvium units
Since the formation is a medium hard rock it has very high strength
characteristics Structurally the tuff is very competent
This formation has relatively low permeability and groundwater is generally not
encountered in this formation When it does occur it is usually in the form of seepage
along joints
In a few borings our exploration encountered volcanic tuff which has weathered
to a saprolite soil In these cases the logs indicate these strata as Saprolite Honolulu
Tuff
Black Sand Rtsp
These soils were developed from volcanic cinder sands laid down by eruptions of
the voicanoes vents of the Honolulu Volcanic Series They
generally form a relatively thin less than 10 feet mantle over coral tuff or basalt and
consist of a surface layer a few feet thick of brown sandy silt or silty sand underiain by
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 16
relatively unweathered cinder sand The sand is usually of medium dense to dense
consistency and is frequently weakly to moderately cemented
Groundwater is usually encountered as a basal water table at low elevations The
cinder sands generally have high rates of permeability making dewatering where
necessary difficult
Recent Alluvium Ra
The Recent Alluvium consists mainly of very soft to medium stiff brown to dark
gray clayey silt and organic silt with varying amounts of sand and infrequent pockets of
gravel and cobbies
These soils are generally the result of sedimentation in a very low energy
environment such as an estuary or bay Frequently the soils assigned to this unit
accumulated very rapidly under water and have never been exposed Therefore there
is a tendency to encounter underconsolidated sediments which settle under the weight
of a minimal overburden or structural loading
Included in this unit are subunits of localized thin layers about 10 feet of lagoonal
marine sediments consisting mostly of very soft to medium stiff gray clayey silts These
gray silts are identified as lagoonal facies subunits of the young alluvial unit
The soils assigned to this unit are generally considered to be poorly to very poorly
suited for use as support for foundation loads due to their low in situ strength
characteristics and the tendency for settlement to occur in structures overlying soils of this
unit
GEOLABS HAWAI
W O 2636 00 Page 17
The following subunits will be used to describe Recent Alluvium
Estuarine Sediments Recent Alluvium
This unit generally consists of dark gray and brown silts and clays in the
consistency range of very soft to soft This soil have also contain traces of fine
sands They are also characterized by a weak to moderate organic odor and also
may contain small fragile mollusk shells
Estuarine Peat Recent Alluvium
This unit generally consists of dark gray and brown silts and clays in the
consistency range of very soft to soft This subunit is differentiated from Estuarine
Sediments by the presence of fibrous organic material This soil also contains
traces of fine to medium grained sands They are also characterized by a
moderate to strong organic odor and also may contain small fragile mollusk shells
although these are usually rare
Lagoonal Deiritus Recent Alluvium
This unit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or siity fine
sand Gravel sized fragments are rare Frequenily the silts are organic in origin
and may have a mild to strong fishy odor It is differentiated from the Lagoonal
Reef Formation by the predominance of sand sized material and the lack of gravel
sized fragments Close examination of the coarse fragments will usually show a
limy coating on all surfaces
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 18
Lagoonal Sediments Recent Alluvium
This unit generally consists of light gray to medium gray silts and clays Gravel
and sand sized fragments are rare Frequently the silts are organic in origin and
may have a mild to strong fishy odor It is differentiated from Estuarine Sediments
by the lighter color and a relatively high content of calcium carbonate
Recent Sand Rs
This mapping unit represents recent deposits of beach and near shore marine
sands These sands are generally composed of medium grained fragments derived from
the coral aigal reefs which have been reduced in size and rounded by water action The
consistency of the sands is generally on the order of medium dense to dense
These recent sands are generally capable of supporting moderately heavy loads
in their natural state Densification of the sands usually improves their bearing capacity
Man Made Fill Rf
This mapping unit represents low areas which were filled during urbanization The
majority of these low areas are underlain at relatively shallow depths by Recent Alluvium
and were originally marshy areas A small part of the filled lands are underlain by coral
The quality and composition of the fill materials are variable These fills were
frequently placed without control Therefore it is difficult to make any general statement
regarding their engineering properties or construction in these areas
Groundwater in the fill areas is generally expected to occur as a basal water table
near sea jevel However higher perched water tables may be encountered
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 19
Along most of Dillingham Boulevard and in parts of Waikiki it appears that the
man made fills are underlain at relatively shallow depths by coral algal limestone and
related deposits These fill materials range from clays to gravels and intermediate
mixtures of these soils Their consistencies range from soft or loose to hard or very
dense
The capability of these man made fills to support structural loads is partly
dependent upon the design loads and the quality of the fill and its placement and partly
dependent upon the materials underlying the fills The actual fill materials may be of
suspect quality
For areas with shallow fills overlying coral algal formation it is our opinion that
foundation ioads may be supported by relatively deep spread footings and or short pile
foundations bearing on the coral algal formation
Quite frequently in areas underlain by Recent Alluvium fills were only intended to
support very light structures on shallow spread footings and therefore only form a thin
crust over the soft alluvial materials
Along Pearl Harbor and at the mouths of major streams man made fills were
generally placed over soft sediments for development along the shoreline areas The fill
materiais range from clays to gravels and intermediate mixtures of soils The
consistencies of the fills range from soft or loose to hard or very dense
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 20
FOUNDATION SUPPORT
General
Our exploration indicates that the proposed transit alignment wili cross a wide
variety of subsurface soil conditions Therefore several different foundation systems will
probably be required to support the fixed guideway system Therefore it is critical that
additional more detailed geotechnical engineering exploration analyses and
recommendations be performed as part of the final design of the proposed transit system
In general it is our opinion that at least three 3 different types of foundation
support systems will be required to support the fixed guideway system and related
structures such as passenger stations The applicability of a particular system will depend
upon site specific subsurface conditions Estimations of which system may be most
appropriate for specific portions of the alignment are presented in a subsequent section
of this report Appendix B
For the purpose of developing preliminary recommendations for foundation support
of the proposed mass transit system and related structures we conducted geotechnical
engineering analyses using the following load parameters provided by ICF Kaiser
Engineers
1 Axial Column Loads 624 to 1 389 kips
2 Transverse Moment 1 778 to 6 826 kip feet
3 Longitudinal Moment 480 to 3 415 kip feet
The magnitude of these load parameters will necessitate the construction of
foundations which have sufficient bearing capacity and rigidity to resist the anticipated
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 21
loads For our preliminary analyses we have assumed a maximum tolerable foundation
settlement of about one 1 inch
Spread Footing Foundations Aerial Rail Piers
In general with the exception of the area between about Station 94 00 to about
Station 111 00 where the proposed fixed guideway system will be on grade our
analyses indicate that spread footing foundations for the aerial portions of the system will
only function properly if they bear on competent in situ rock formations i e basalt or tuff
with a minimum rock thickness of ten 10 feet below the base of the footing Based on
our preliminary analyses spread footing foundations bearing on very stiff to hard alluvial
and residual soils may experience settlements on the order to two 2 to three 3 inches
total Settlements of this magnitude are generally considered intolerable
For preliminary design purposes a maximum allowable bearing capacity of 10 000
pounds per square foot may be used for the design of spread footing foundations bearing
on competent in situ rock formations The base of the foundations should be embedded
a minimum of five 5 feet below the lowest adjacent grade Spread footing foundations
located in sloping areas should be further embedded if necessary so that there is a
minimum of fifteen 15 feet of setback from the toe of the footing out horizontally to the
nearest slope face or should be embedded to the same elevation as the toe of the slope
whichever is less Spread footing foundations located adjacent to retaining walls should
be embedded to an elevation at least as low as the lowest adjacent elevation of the
retaining wall foundations
Spread Footing Foundations On grade Structures
In the area between about Station 94 00 to about Station 111 00 the proposed
fixed guideway system will be on grade The Waiawa passenger station will be an on
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 22
grade siructure It is anticipated that this structure will be one to two stories in height and
will be constructed of reinforced concrete or masonry units
Based on our exploration in this area an allowable bearing capacity of 3 000
pounds per square foot on stiff alluvial or residual soil may be utilized for the preliminary
design of the foundations for the passenger station Ifthe soils are loosened or disturbed
during construction the foundation subgrade soils should be scarified moisture
conditioned to about three 3 percent above optimum moisture content and recompacted
to 95 of the maximum dry density as determined by ASTM Test Method D 1557 78
Foundations should be embedded a minimum of 24 inches below the lowest adjacent
grade
Drilled Pier Foundations
In areas where very stiff to hard residual and alluvial subsoils are encountered
drilled pier or caisson foundations may be utilized to support the proposed rail system
and related structures
The vertical load capacities of the drilled piers will be derived primarily by frictional
resistance between the shait of the pier and the surrounding soils or rock and partially
from end bearing on competent soil or rock formations
Design curves illustrating the relationship between the size of pier depth of pier
and soil conditions with vertical load capacity of the pier are presented on Plate C 1
Due to the high lateral loads and overturning moment requirements anticipated for
the aerial system and related structures it is our opinion that relatively large diameter
drilled piers should be used
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 23
Lateral resistance of the drilled piers would be derived from the horizontal shear
strength of the surrounding soils or rock and also the bending movement for which the
piers are design The lateral resistance of the drilled piers are addressed in a subsequent
section
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered in our preliminary exploration we
estimate that for areas where drilled pier foundations are feasible the piers should be a
minimum of 3 0 feet in diameter and should generally penetrate sufficiently below the
existing ground surface in order to develop adequate vertical and lateral capacity to resist
the given loads with an appropriate factor of safety Depending upon site specific
conditions it may be possible to reduce the size and embedment of the piers where very
competent basalt coral or tuff is encountered at shallow depths
Since a significant portion of the load carrying capacity of the drilled piers comes
from frictional resistance between the perimeter of the pier and the surrounding in situ
soils or rocks it is critical that the pier bore is drilled properly It is also critical that an
appropriate concrete mix design is selected and that the concrete is placed properly in
the drilled shaft
During the drilling of the shaft the use of drilling fluids should be avoided as much
as practicable since the fluids may saturate and weaken the subsoils or rock thereby
reducing the shear strength of the surrounding materials This reduction in shear strength
would result in a loss of both vertical and lateral load bearing capacity for the drilled pier
foundations As much as possible the holes should be advanced by auger drilling or
other dry methods
The concrete mix should be designed to minimize shrinkage of the concrete after
installation in the drilled shaft in order to maintain a high degree of contact between the
concrete and the surrounding soils or rock The concrete should be placed in the drilled
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 24
shait as soon as possible after completion of drilling to minimize the potential of the shaft
caving in The concrete should be placed by a method that minimizes the potential of
aggregate segregation and minimizes the potential of bridging Careful records should
be kept of the volume of each hole and compared to records of the volumes of concrete
placed there in This will facilitate timely determination of discrepancies which may be
caused by bridging cave ins or loss of concrete into cavities or through fissures
In some areas other factors may limit the use of drilled piers where artesian
groundwater is encountered at relatively shallow depths it may be difficult to properly
place the concrete into the drilled holes As discussed in a subsequent section the soils
in some portions of the alignment may be contaminated by fuel products Drilled piers
may not be desirable since excavated soils in these areas may be require special
handling and disposal
Pile Foundation
In areas underlain by soft to stiff subsoils and with high groundwater levels we
anticipate that deep pile foundation systems will be required to support the fixed guideway
system piers Based upon our study we anticipate that in many of these areas bearing
strata may be encountered at depths ranging from about 50 to 150 feet below the existing
ground surface
Where hard basalt rock or coral formation is present for end bearing support
vertical pile capacities of 100 to 150 tons may be used depending on the thickness and
hardness of the bearing layer
However there are many areas particularly around Pearl Harbor where the hard
bearing strata are at depths of 150 to 350 feet In these areas friction piles may be
driven to a sufficient depth to develop the vertical load capacity from adhesion between
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 25
the piles and the surrounding soils The vertical load capacity of the pile would depend
on the pile perimeter area available soil adhesion and the length of the pile driven The
relationship of these factors to pile capacity are presented on Plates C 2 through C 5
Laterai capacity of the piles would be developed by interaction between the piles
and the upper soils In general higher lateral capacity may be attainable in denser or stiff
near surface subsoils or soft subsoils overlain by a dense or stiff crust The lateral
resistance of the piles is addressed in a subsequent section
Pile Size
Two 2 sizes of piles were used in our analyses 16 5 inch octagonal and 20 inch
square prestressed concrete piles The 16 5 inch pile is the most commonly used
pile in Hawaii and is readily available in large quantities However it appears that
it may be desirable to use 20 inch square piles for this project due their higher
lateral resistance and larger perimeter area resulting in higher frictional capacity
The large number of piles that would be required for the project would tend to
offset disadvantages that might be experienced in procuring this special size of
precast pile
Predrilling
Predrilling through stiff surface crusts may be required in some areas prior to pile
driving to penetrate locally hard fill layers or boulders that may be present and to
minimize disturbance to existing utilities along to the alignment It is estimated that
the predrilling generally would not be deeper than about 5 to 10 feet
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 26
Pile Driving Hammer
Relatively large pile driving hammers with a minimum driving energy of about
45 000 foot pounds may be required for the long lengths of piles and or hard
driving resistances anticipated The hammer stroke or energy should be carefully
controlled during driving in soft or loose soils so that excessive tensile stresses are
not developed in the pile
Downdrag
in the vicinity of the Keehi Interchange there are deposits of soft soils which are
settling under the influence of embankments placed several years ago Deep pile
foundations will be required in this area
The continuing settlement of the soft material due to adhesion between the soil
and the pile will induce vertical loads on the piles in addition to the structural
loads We estimate that these downdrag loads will be on the order of
approximately 150 tons per pile For foundation design the downdrag forces must
be added to the structural loads applied to the piles
Additionally some means to isolate the piles from the settling soil or to reduce the
downdrag loads may be required
Downdrag loads are generally not anticipated in other areas along the alignment
unless significant fills are planned over soft soil deposits
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 27
Lateral Pile Pier Capacity
The lateral resistance of the foundations are a critical factor in the design of the
foundations of the aerial structures It appears that the lateral resistance of the
piles or piers may have a greater influence on the foundation design than the
vertical pile capacity
The lateral resistance of a pile or pier depends on the soil strength diameter of pile
or pier stiffness of the pile and fixity of the top of the pile or pier
The COM 622 computer program was utilized for our lateral pile pier capacity
analysis This program utilizes the finite difference method of analysis where non
linear stress strain relationship of soil can be used Based on the soil parameters
developed and the laboratory stress strain curves representative p y curves were
developed for each major soil category for input into the program
Since the lateral soil modulus varies with deflection experienced the computerized
analysis first assumes a moduius value then calculates the pile deflection based
on the assumed modulus The calculated deflection is then used to determine the
modulus from the p y curves The calculated modulus and the assumed modulus
are then compared The procedure is repeated until the newly assumed modulus
and calculated moduli are essentially the same
The results of the analyses are presented on Plates C 6 through C 49 as curves
for each of the soil catergory indicating the pile pier deflection at the pile pier
cap versus the horizontal force and curves indicating the calculated pile pier
moment versus horizontal force for each soil category The effects of group
action are also included in the appropriate plates
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 28
Anticipated Foundation Systems
The subsurface data from the exploration and the foundation engineering analyses
were combined to provided a general delineation of anticipated foundation systems that
may be appropriate for the project The final details of the foundation system will depend
on the aciual location and loading of the piers therefore these foundation alternaiives are
presented only as a guide The delineation of the possible foundation systems are
presented in Appendix B
Existing Utilities
There are numerous existing utility lines buried below the proposed alignment
particularly along Kamehameha Highway in the Pearl City and Aiea areas Construction
of the foundations for the aerial structures may require relocation or rerouting of these
existing utilities
ANTICIPATED SURFACE CONDITIONS
The majority of the proposed transit alignment is situated in developed or
urbanized areas Therefore surface conditions may be congested but are generally
underlain by relatively competent surface materials or at least a relatively stable crust
overlying poor subsoil conditions In most areas the surface soils would be sufficiently
competent to support construction activities
CONSTRUCTION CONSIDERATIONS
Excavation
For the aerial portions of the proposed transit system some excavation will be
required to allow installation of pile caps and related underground structures Based on
our review of available data it is anticipated that both soft ground and hard excavation
will be encountered along the proposed alignment
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 29
Most of the proposed transit system is situated in highly developed urban areas
with a significant number of existing facilities along and adjacent to the alignment The
system contractor should have sufficient experience and capability for construction in a
congested urban environment
It is anticipated that most of the excavation will have to be braced shored or
otherwise stabilized to reduce the potential of lateral movement of the excavation wails
Such movement could destabilize the subsoils supporting adjacent facilities and it could
potentially cause settlement and or other damage The possible stabilization techniques
include but are not limited to the installation of temporary interlocking sheeting and
shoring the sloping of the excavation sidewalls for temporary stability and or grouting
of the subsoils to create a competent stable mass
The methods and techniques of stabilization are the responsibility of the contractor
who has to consider the applicable regulations and safe construction practices It is
recommended that a preconstruction damage survey be conducted to establish
preexisting conditions adjacent to any excavation to reduce the potential for unjustified
claims It is further recommended that a surveillance program be implemented during
construction to monitor for potential signs of instability
Groundwater Conditions
The project area is located within the Pearl Harbor and Honolulu artesian aquifer
basins As previously discussed artesian groundwater occurs within the Koolau Basalt
The potentiometric head of this aquifer varies spatially temporally and in response to
pumpage Generally at any location where the foundations encounter Koolau Basalt at
an elevation of less than 20 feet there is a significant chance of encountering artesian
groundwaier
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 30
In addition groundwater is also encountered in the other geologic units described
within the project area Generally this groundwater occurs as a basal water table at or
near sea level This groundwater is usuaily brackish or saline In some isolated areas
perched groundwater and or sub artesian groundwater may be encountered Other
excavations which penetrate to or below sea level will most likely encounter groundwater
Dewatering
Based on our review of the available data it is our anticipation that many of the
excavations for pile caps drilled pier foundations and related underground
structures will encounter groundwater levels within their excavation limits
Therefore dewatering of the excavations will be required to allow construction to
be constructed in dry conditions
It is anticipated that excavations in areas underlain by coral formations will
encounter highiy permeable subsoil conditions which will require extensive
pumping units to dewater the excavations unless additional precautions are taken
to limit the flow of groundwater into the open excavation
Dewatering in soft soil conditions may cause an areal drawdown of the
groundwater levels in the area surrounding the excavation This areal drawdown
could result in a loss of the buoyant forces which are partially supporting the soft
subsoils and any facilities situated in or on the surface of the soft soils This loss
of support could potentially cause settlement of the soft soils and potentially result
in damage to the existing improvements
The techniques and methods of dewatering are the responsibility of the contractor
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 31
GEOENVIRONMENTAL CONSIDERATIONS
During our field exploration strong hydrocarbon odors were noticed in soil sampies
in one of the borings along Dillingham Boulevard The occurrence of these hydrocarbon
odors is noted on the boring logs in Appendix A We suspect that these odors may be
the result of contamination of the subsoils by a fuel product such as diesei fuel or
gasoline
Although it is not in our scope of work to determine the nature and extent of the
suspected contamination nor is environmental consulting our speciality we feel that we
must point out the potential implications of encountering fuel contamination during
construction Based on our understanding of the current laws and regulations the
following impacts are possible
1 Depending upon the actual level of contamination of the subsoils the
excavated soil may require special handling treatment or disposal In the
worst case situation the excavated material could be considered to be a
hazardous waste and would have to be disposed of in a manner consistent
with that required for hazardous materials
2 Generally if the soils near the groundwater table are contaminated the
groundwater may be assumed to be also contaminated Again depending
upon the level of contamination the water encountered during construction
may require special handling treatment or disposal It is most likely that
even at relatively low levels of contamination it would not be permissible to
discharge the water generated during dewatering operations without some
form of treatment
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 32
3 The fumes and vapors of fuel products in certain concentrations are highly
flammabie and in lower concentrations are considered harmful if inhaled
Depending upon the level of contamination special efforts may be needed
to protect workers during the construction activities
ADDITIONAL EXPLORATION
The subsurface conditions along the currently proposed alignment are highly
variable and complex The discussions and recommendations presented in this report
are general in nature
It is recommended that a full scale geotechnical exploration program including
borings field and laboratory testing and technical evaluation and analyses be developed
and implemented prior to the final design stage of the subject project The
recommendations presented herein are contingent upon the performance of such an
exploration by a competent geotechnical engineer
LIMITATIONS
The analyses and recommendations submitted in this report are based in part upon
information obtained from borings and research of available literature and data Variations
of conditions may occur and the nature and extent of these variations may not become
evident until a detailed field exploration is performed or even uniil construction is
underway If variations then appear evident it will be necessary to re evaluate the
recommendations given in this report
This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of the City and County of
Honolulu for specific application to the proposed Honolulu Rapid Transit Project in
accordance with generally accepted geotechnical engineering principles and practices
No other warranty expressed or implied is made
GEOLABS HAWAII
W O 2636 00 Page 33
A contractor wishing to bid on this project is urged to retain a competent
geotechnical engineer to assist in the interpretation of this report

APPENDIX A
Field Exploration
The subsurface conditions along the proposed aerial portions of the transit
alignment were explored by the drilling and sampling of fifty nine 69 borings to depths
ranging between 18 0 and 200 0 feet below the existing ground surface at the
approximate locations shown on the Site Plans Plates 2 through 35 The borings were
drilled by five 5 truck mounted rotary drill rigs advancing continuous flight augers or
rotary and core drilling tools as appropriate The entire field exploration involved a total
of approximately 5 670 lineal feet of drilling and sampling Boring 47 and 55
were omitted from the exploration since subsurface information was available from other
sources
The materials encountered in the borings were examined and classified in general
accordance with ASTM Standard Practice D 2488 84 Description and Identification of
Soils Visual Manual Procedure by field engineers and geologists from our firm The
logs of the borings are attached in this appendix
Soil samples were obtained from the drilled borings in general accordance with
ASTM Standard Method D 1586 84 Penetration Test and Split Barrel Sampling of Soils
sampler with a 140 pound hammer falling 30 inches The blow counts to drive the
sampler the last 12 inches are shown on the boring logs at the appropriate sample
depths
In soft soil conditions soil samples were obtained by the use of thin wailed tube
sampiers Shelby tubes in general accordance with ASTM Standard Practice D 1587 83
Thin walled Tube Sampling of Soils using both 2 5 and 3 inch diameter tubes The
samples were obtained by pushing the samplers into the in situ soils using the hydraulic
feed system of the drilling rig The hydraulic pressure shown on the feed gauge as
required to advance the tube sampler per 6 inch increment are shown on the boring logs
at the appropriate sample depths
Core samples of soil and rock materials encountered at the site were obtained
using diamond core drilling techniques in general accordance with ASTM Standard
Practice D 2113 83 Diamond Core Drilling for Site Investigation Core drilling is a rotary
drilling method which uses a hollow bit to cut into the rock formation The rock material
left in the annulus of the bit is mechanically recovered for examination classification and
testing
GEOLABS HAWAII
Appendix A Continued Page 2
Recovery REC is used as a subjective guide to the interpretation of the relative
quality of rock masses Recovery is defined as the actual lengih of material recovered
from a coring attempt vs the length of the core attempt e g if 3 7 feet of materiai is
recovered from a 5 0 foot core run the recovery would be 74 and would be shown on
the Boring Logs as REC 74
The Rock Quality Designation RQD is also a subjective guide to the relative
quality of rock masses This designation is only applicable to cores retrieved from in situ
rock formations RQD is defined as the percentage of the core run that is sound material
that is in excess of four 4 inches in length without any discontinuities discounting any
drilling induced fractures or breaks i e if 2 5 feet of sound material is recovered from a
5 0 foot core run the RQD would be 50 and wouid be shown on the Boring Logs as
RQD 50 Generally the following is used to describe the relative rock quality based
on the Practical Handbook of Physical Properties of Rocks and Minerals
Rock Quality RQD
Very Poor 0 25
Poor 25 50
Fair 50 75
Good 75 90
Excellent 90 100
The rippability or foundation support characteristics of a rock mass is a function
of the relative hardness of the rock its relative quality brittleness and _ fissile
characteristics A dense basalt with a high RQD would be very difficult to rip and wouid
probably require blasting or splitting for excavation
GEOLABS HAWAII

APPENDIX B
Generalized Foundation Conditions
eee en nee eee
The subsurface information obtained from review of available literature general
knowledge of the geology along the route grained through previous experience site
reconnaissance and findings obtained from field borings and laboratory testing of
selected representative samples were evaluated to determine approximately
delineations of areas which may be anticipated to have similar subsurface
geotechnical conditions upon which more refined foundation design may be based
The estimated foundation loads were then utilized to determine the types of
potential foundation systems which may be most applicable for the areas thus
identified
lt should be recognized that the applicability of the various foundation systems
will depend on the design loads as finally determined based on the criteria of the
aerial transit system selected Therefore potential vendors of the transit system
should evaluate the specific loading criteria for their system in conjunction with the
anticipated subsurface conditions to determine the applicability of the various
foundation systems considered
It is also recognized that other conditions such as overhead obstructions
buried utility lines and existing structures in the vicinity may result in imposition of
restrictions on the type s and size s of construction equipment which may be used
and therefore will also have a bearing on the applicability and cost effectiveness of
the particular foundation system which would otherwise be considered most
appropriate
The areas anticipated to have similar foundation conditions and the foundation
systems which may be considered to be appropriate are presented on Plates B 1
through B 29

APPENDIX C
Preliminary Design Curves for Deep Foundations
Engineering analyses were conducted utilizing general soil parameters to
develop design curves for determination of the vertical and lateral pile and pier
capacities These curves are presented for use as a guide in determining the
foundation system that may be required
Vertical Pile and Pier Capacity Analysis
The vertical capacity of a pile is given by the sum of the frictional resistance
between the soil and pile or pier shaft and the base resistance generated at the tip
of the pile or pier
For the evaluation of the shaft friction the method represented by the
following equation was adopted
Ca Su
Where Ca is the shaft friction per unit area
Su is the undrained shear strength of soil and
is a dimensionless parameter dependent on Su
In general varies between 0 5 to 1 0 and its value usually increases
with decrease of Su
For the evaluation of the base resistance the equation used is represented as
follows
Ba
q cNc D Nqg Nv
Where Nc Ng and Nw are functions of the friction angle and their values
were determined from Navy Design Manual 7 2 The last term 2b Nz is normally
ignored due to its relatively small contribution to the vertical capacity
Cis the cohesion of the soil
Dz is the overburden pressure and
Bis the diameter of the pile
The vertical pile capacity was evaluated based on the above two equations
Plates C 1 through C 5 present the vertical pile capacity with pile tip elevation for
various size piles for each soil category
GEOLABS HAWAII
Appendix C Continued
Lateral Pile and Pier Analysis
Lateral pile and pier analyses for this investigation were performed utilizing a
computer program PILEDG which is a PC version of the main frame program
COM622 developed at the University of Texas at Austin
Both soil and structural parameters are required for compiling the computer
input The structural input includes
1 Pile or pier dimensions
2 material properties of pile or pier and
3 structural loadings e g axial and lateral
loadings and or bending moments
The soil input consists of soil parameters such as friction angle or undrained
shear strength of soil which will be used by the program to generate p y curves at
selected depths along the pile or pier A p y curve represents the relationship
between the lateral loading on the pile or pier and the lateral displacement of the soil
The relationship is non linear and may be generalized as
P C yY
where C varies with depth and is a function of soil stress strain relationship
Generally the lateral capacity of a long pile or pier is governed by its lateral
deflection and bending moment induced by the lateral loading
Our analysis was carried out for long piles or piers embedded in various
subsoil conditions typically encountered along the alignment The magnitude of the
lateral loading was varied to evaluate the different amounts of lateral pile deflection
and bending moment induced in the piles or piers
The results of the analyses are presented as curves of the lateral deflection at
the top of pile or pier versus lateral load and the induced bending moment in the pile
or pier versus lateral load Fixed head condition and the effect of group action were
evaluated
The preliminary design curves are presented on Plates C 6 through C 49
W O 2636 00 MARCH 1991

ADDENDUM NO 6
APPENDIX D
Laboratory Testing
Laboratory testing was performed on selected samples obtained during our field
exploration A laboratory testing program was developed to schedule appropriate tests
to aid in determining pertinent engineering properties of the soil and or rock materials
sampled
Classification Tests
Frequently the classification of a material provides a correlative index to certain
engineering properties of the material
Moisture Content ASTM Test Method D 2216 80 and Unit Weight Determinations
are used as an aid to soil classification and to aid in determining certain properties such
as relative saturation The moisture content expressed as a percentage of the dry weight
of the tested sampie is shown at the appropriate location on plates summarizing test
results and at the appropriate sample depths on the Boring Logs
Atterberg Limits tests ASTM Test Method D 4318 84 are used to determine the
plasticity of the fine grained portion of soils The Plasticity Index of the material is used
to classify the soil under the Unified Soil Classification System The results of these tests
are shown at the appropriate sample depths on the Boring Logs and are summarized on
Plates D 1 through D 158
Sieve Analysis tests ASTM Test Method D 422 63 are performed to determine the
grain size distribution of the soil The grain size distribution is used for classification in
the Unified Soil Classification System and to provide indices on other engineering
properties The results of these tests are summarized on Plates D 1 through D 158
Physical Property Tests
Tests are performed to deveiop a correlative index of certain physical parameters
of a soil These physical parameters may be used in engineering analyses pertaining to
the performance or suitability of a soil
Direct Shear tests ASTM Test Method D 3080 72 are used to determine the angle
of internal friction and the coefficient of cohesion in a soil sample These data are used
in various engineering caiculations The results of the direct shear tests are presented
on Plates D 159 through D 1771
Unconsolidated Undrained Triaxial Compression tests ASTM Test Method D 2850
87 are performed to evaluate the shear strength parameters of the soils The resuits of
these tests are summarized on Plates D 172 through D 218
GEOLABS HAWAII
Appendix D Continued Page 2
Unconfined Compression tests ASTM Test Method D 2938 79 were performed
on selected rock core samples The ends of the rock core samples were cut with a
diamond saw parallel to each other and at right angles to the longitudinal axis of the core
The axial lengths of the samples were 2 0 to 2 5 times its diameter The load was applied
continuously at a rate of 0 05 inches minute until failure occurred The stress at failure
are presented on Plates D 1 through D 158
Torvane Shear tests are used to determine the coefficient of cohesion in a soil
sample The results of the torvane shear tests are presented on Plates D 1 through
D 158
Consolidation tests ASTM Test Method D 2435 80 are used to evaluate the
consolidation characteristics of the soil The results of these tests are summarized on
Piates D 219 through D 228
